## Trust Assumptions & Verification

### 🛡️ 安全运行的前提条件

以下为当前 skill 的核心信任假设。未通过验证的条件需要人工确认，否则相关风险不应降级。

1. ❌ **The npm account associated with NPM_TOKEN belongs to the legitimate maintainer (Erik Vullings) and has not been compromised** *(confidence: 30%)*
   - 检验结果：Insufficient data for npmjs.com account for mithril-ui-form — manual check recommended.
   - 关联主体：`npmjs.com account for mithril-ui-form`

2. ❌ **The GitHub Actions environment for the erikvullings/mithril-ui-form repository is not compromised, and no malicious third-party actions are injected into the workflow** *(confidence: 30%)*
   - 检验结果：Insufficient data for GitHub Actions workflow — .github/workflows/release.yml — manual check recommended.
   - 关联主体：`GitHub Actions workflow — .github/workflows/release.yml`

3. ❌ **GitHub Actions pinned actions (actions/checkout@v4, actions/setup-node@v4, pnpm/action-setup@v4) correspond to their official, uncompromised versions** *(confidence: 30%)*
   - 检验结果：Insufficient data for GitHub Actions: actions/checkout@v4, actions/setup-node@v4, pnpm/action-setup@v4 — manual check recommended.
   - 关联主体：`GitHub Actions: actions/checkout@v4, actions/setup-node@v4, pnpm/action-setup@v4`

4. ❌ **The mithril-ui-form library's form rendering implementation does not use eval(), innerHTML with unsanitized user data, or dynamic function construction from JSON schema values** *(confidence: 30%)*
   - 检验结果：Insufficient data for mithril-ui-form runtime rendering code (not provided for review) — manual check recommended.
   - 关联主体：`mithril-ui-form runtime rendering code (not provided for review)`

5. ❌ **The markdown rendering implementation used by mithril-ui-form sanitizes HTML output to prevent XSS injection** *(confidence: 30%)*
   - 检验结果：Insufficient data for Markdown renderer used within mithril-ui-form (library not identified in provided files) — manual check recommended.
   - 关联主体：`Markdown renderer used within mithril-ui-form (library not identified in provided files)`

6. ❌ **registry.npmjs.org is the official npm registry and has not been subject to DNS hijacking or BGP route manipulation at time of publish** *(confidence: 0%)*
   - 检验结果：Found 0 source(s) for registry.npmjs.org. Confidence: 0%. Could not confidently confirm — manual check recommended.
   - 关联主体：`https://registry.npmjs.org`

7. ❌ **registry is a reputable, widely-used service** *(confidence: 30%)*
   - 检验结果：Insufficient data for registry — manual check recommended.
   - 关联主体：`registry`

## LLM Cross-Validation Results

### Analysis Group #1

- **Final Severity**: SAFE
- **Confidence**: 78%
- **Is Malicious**: False
- **Is Gray Area**: True

#### Gray Area Analysis

## Gray Area Analysis

### Why this might be legitimate:
- This configuration is used by the semantic-release tool to automate the package release process. When CI/CD runs, it analyzes commit messages to determine version bumps, generates changelogs, publishes to npm, commits updated files back to the repository, and creates GitHub releases.
- This file is used by the repository's CI/CD pipeline to automatically determine the next semantic version number, generate release notes, update the CHANGELOG.md, publish the package to npm, and create a GitHub release based on standardized commit messages.
- The correct/safe usage is to run this only in trusted CI on protected branches, with tightly scoped tokens (npm publish token limited to the intended package/org, GitHub token with minimal repo permissions). The plugin order here is normal: analyze commits -> generate notes -> update changelog -> publish to npm -> commit release files -> create GitHub release. Using prerelease channels (`alpha`, `beta`, `rc`) is also safe when branch naming and release channels are intentionally mapped and documented. The `[skip ci]` in the git commit message is a valid pattern to prevent release-commit loops. Safe operation depends on branch protection (required reviews/status checks), commit convention enforcement, and controlled access to who can push to release branches.

### Why this might be dangerous:
- In its current state, this file cannot be exploited. However, if an attacker were to gain write access to the repository, they could modify this file to include the `@semantic-release/exec` plugin to execute malicious shell commands during the trusted CI/CD release process, potentially stealing npm publish tokens or injecting malware into the build artifacts before they are published.
- This pattern becomes dangerous if CI credentials are over-privileged or branch protections are weak. For example, if an attacker can push to `main`/`master`/`beta`/`rc`/`alpha` or compromise CI secrets, they can trigger automatic npm/GitHub releases containing malicious code. Including `@semantic-release/git` means CI can write back to the repository; if token permissions are broad, that write capability can be abused for unauthorized commits/tags. Another risk is accidental branch misuse: commits on prerelease branches can unintentionally publish externally visible prerelease packages. Missing safeguards that would reduce risk include strict branch protection rules, least-privilege tokens, environment protection gates for publish steps, and mandatory signed/verified commits or release provenance controls.

### Recommendations:
- [security_analyst] No action required. This is a standard, safe CI/CD release configuration file with no security concerns.
- [adversarial_analyst] No changes are needed for this file. To protect the release pipeline, ensure strict branch protection rules are enforced on all release branches (master, main, beta, rc, alpha) and adhere to the principle of least privilege for CI/CD secrets (like NPM_TOKEN and GITHUB_TOKEN).
- [intent_analyst] Keep this config, but harden release security: (1) enforce branch protection and restrict push/merge rights on all listed release branches, (2) use separate least-privilege tokens for npm and GitHub with rotation and secret scanning, (3) gate publishing jobs with protected environments/manual approvals for stable releases, (4) enforce conventional commits and required CI checks, (5) consider npm provenance/2FA and signed tags/releases, and (6) monitor release events and package integrity (e.g., lock down who can modify CI workflows).

It defines release branches (master, main, beta, rc, alpha) and a standard set of semantic-release plugins for commit analysis, release notes generation, changelog updates, npm publishing, git tagging, and GitHub release creation. All plugins used (@semantic-release/commit-analyzer, @semantic-release/release-notes-generator, @semantic-release/changelog, @semantic-release/npm, @semantic-release/git, @semantic-release/github) are well-known, official semantic-release plugins with no suspicious behavior.

### Analysis Group #2

- **Final Severity**: SAFE
- **Confidence**: 100%

#### Gray Area Analysis

## Gray Area Analysis

### Why this might be legitimate:
- This lock file is used by pnpm to ensure reproducible installs of the project's dependencies. It pins exact versions of all packages in the dependency tree for a Mithril.js UI form generation library, which is exactly what a lock file is designed to do.
- The correct/safe use of this pattern is to treat the lockfile as an immutable build artifact generated by the package manager and committed to source control. It should be updated only through intentional dependency changes (`pnpm add/update/install`) and reviewed in PRs for unexpected package additions/version jumps. In monorepos, `workspace:*`/`link:` entries are normal and safe when they refer to trusted internal packages. In CI/CD, installs should use `pnpm install --frozen-lockfile` to guarantee reproducibility and prevent unreviewed resolution drift. Teams should also run regular dependency audits, pin Node/pnpm versions, and use trusted registries and provenance/signature checks where available.
- This is a standard package manager lockfile used to ensure deterministic builds across different environments by pinning exact versions of direct and transitive dependencies.

### Why this might be dangerous:
- This pattern becomes dangerous if the lockfile is tampered with or blindly trusted. Specific risky conditions include: (1) lockfile introduces a compromised transitive package version; (2) dependency confusion via registry misconfiguration or unexpected package name resolution; (3) install-time script abuse from newly introduced dependencies; (4) unnoticed major ecosystem shifts (e.g., test/build tooling upgrades) that pull in risky transitive trees; (5) CI not enforcing `--frozen-lockfile`, allowing drift from reviewed state. In those conditions, the lockfile can act as a supply-chain attack delivery mechanism even though it is not executable application code itself.
- While this specific file is benign, lockfiles are prime targets for 'Lockfile Poisoning'. An attacker who gains commit access could subtly alter the lockfile to pull a legitimate-looking package from a malicious registry, or introduce a typosquatted dependency. Because lockfiles are massive and rarely reviewed line-by-line by humans, the malicious payload would likely be executed automatically during the next CI/CD build or developer `pnpm install` via post-install scripts.

### Recommendations:
- [security_analyst] No action required. This is a normal, legitimate pnpm lock file with no security concerns identified. Standard dependency auditing practices (e.g., running 'pnpm audit') should be maintained as part of regular development workflow.
- [intent_analyst] Keep using a lockfile, but harden supply-chain controls: enforce `pnpm install --frozen-lockfile` in CI, require human review for lockfile diffs, enable Dependabot/Renovate with approval policies, run `pnpm audit`/SCA scans, restrict registry sources, and disable or tightly control lifecycle scripts in CI where possible (`--ignore-scripts` for untrusted contexts). Add provenance/signature verification and branch protections so lockfile changes cannot be merged without review.
- [adversarial_analyst] Maintain strict access controls on the repository. Use automated dependency scanning tools (like `pnpm audit`, Dependabot, or Snyk) to continuously monitor for vulnerable or malicious packages. Enforce lockfile integrity checks in CI/CD pipelines to ensure the lockfile matches the `package.json` and hasn't been tampered with.

It contains dependency specifications for a monorepo with packages including mithril-ui-form, example applications, and plugins. All dependencies are well-known, legitimate packages from the npm ecosystem: semantic-release, rollup, webpack, TypeScript, Jest, jsdom, leaflet, and related tooling. There are no suspicious packages, typosquatted names, or unexpected dependencies. The lock file structure is normal and the package versions appear to be recent, legitimate releases.

## Gray Area Analysis

### Why this might be legitimate:
- This file serves as a Claude skill prompt providing expert guidance for developers working with the mithril-materialized UI component library. It documents architectural patterns, component implementation examples, validation patterns, and development commands — all standard content for a developer-focused AI skill.
- Safe usage of this pattern is: (1) treat `readonly`/`disabled` as UI state only, not a trust control; (2) skip visual validation on blur for non-interactive fields to avoid confusing users; (3) still keep canonical validation in shared business logic and on the server/API; (4) ensure value propagation (`onchange`) from readonly/disabled fields is intentional (e.g., for computed fields) and documented. For the shell block, safe usage is providing non-destructive developer commands with clear context (repo root, expected environment, and what each command does).
- This file is a legitimate AI skill prompt (documentation) designed to teach an LLM how to correctly generate code for the `mithril-materialized` UI library. It provides architectural principles, design patterns, and code examples to improve the AI's coding accuracy.

### Why this might be dangerous:
- This becomes dangerous when specific conditions are true: (a) downstream code assumes that fields not validated on blur are therefore valid; (b) an attacker can toggle `disabled`/`readonly` in DevTools or trigger synthetic events, causing `onchange(getValue(target))` to propagate unvalidated data; (c) server-side validation is missing or inconsistent with client rules; (d) hidden/readonly fields carry sensitive business values (prices, roles, IDs) and are trusted. Missing safeguards include: explicit guard that non-interactive fields are excluded from mutable business inputs, invariant validation before submit, authoritative server-side checks, and clear separation between UI validity state and security validation. For command docs, risk appears only if dangerous commands are added without warnings.
- No realistic abuse scenario exists for this specific code. It is purely benign documentation and standard UI component logic.

### Recommendations:
- [security_analyst] No action required. Both static analysis findings are false positives. The 'prompt_safety_bypass' detection incorrectly pattern-matched on a legitimate code comment within a TypeScript example, and the 'prompt_embedded_shell' detection flagged standard development workflow documentation. The file is safe to use as intended.
- [intent_analyst] Keep the pattern but harden it: add a pre-submit/full-form validation pass that ignores UI interactivity flags; enforce the same validation schema server-side; consider blocking `onchange` emission for `disabled` fields unless explicitly enabled by a prop (e.g., `emitWhenDisabled`); log/warn when readonly/disabled fields are mapped to security-sensitive payload keys; and document that client-side validation is UX-only. For the shell section, keep commands but add a short safety note (non-destructive, run from repo root) and avoid including any destructive one-liners without explicit confirmation steps.
- [adversarial_analyst] Mark these findings as false positives. To reduce future noise, consider tuning the static analysis rules to contextualize keywords like 'validation' (e.g., ignoring them when inside code blocks or referring to HTML elements) and allowing standard package manager commands (`npm`, `pnpm`) in markdown documentation blocks.

#### Gray Area Analysis

## Gray Area Analysis

### Why this might be legitimate:
- This skill file is used to provide an AI coding assistant (such as Claude) with structured knowledge about the mithril-ui-form library so it can help developers create, configure, and troubleshoot Mithril-based forms. It documents JSON schema syntax, available field types, conditional logic, plugins, and accessibility features.
- The safe and correct usage pattern is to treat form schemas as trusted configuration, validate all input/output at the application boundary, and enforce server-side controls independent of UI rules. In practice: (1) only allow approved field types/plugins from a whitelist, (2) sanitize any rendered rich content such as markdown (`type: md`) before injecting into the DOM, (3) validate uploaded files by MIME/type/size/content on the backend even if `options` are set client-side, (4) treat `show`, `required`, and other schema constraints as UX helpers—not security controls, (5) enforce strict schema validation (e.g., JSON schema + TypeScript types) before rendering dynamic forms, and (6) use least-privilege endpoints for form submission/upload APIs with authn/authz checks and rate limits.
- This is a legitimate skill definition used to provide context to an AI assistant, enabling it to help developers write or troubleshoot JSON configurations for the `mithril-ui-form` framework.

### Why this might be dangerous:
- This becomes dangerous when untrusted users can supply or modify schema JSON, plugin registration, or rich content without sanitization and policy checks. Specific risk conditions include: user-controlled markdown leading to stored/DOM XSS if rendered unsafely; permissive plugin loading enabling arbitrary component behavior or data exfiltration; file upload fields relying only on client hints and allowing malicious files; conditional field hiding (`show`) used to suppress sensitive fields while backend still accepts hidden values (parameter tampering); and weak ID/autogeneration assumptions causing collisions or unauthorized object overwrites in multi-tenant data flows. The pattern is especially risky if schema is fetched remotely at runtime, merged dynamically, or executed in admin panels without integrity checks.
- While the prompt itself is completely benign, an attacker interacting with an AI using this skill could attempt to trick the LLM into generating malicious form configurations. For example, they might ask the AI to create a form where the file upload `url` points to an attacker-controlled server, or inject XSS payloads into the `md` (Markdown) or `label` fields. If the host application blindly renders the AI-generated JSON without sanitization, it could lead to Cross-Site Scripting (XSS) or data exfiltration.

### Recommendations:
- [security_analyst] No action required. This file is safe and serves its documented purpose as a reference skill for AI-assisted development with the mithril-ui-form library.
- [intent_analyst] Keep this architecture, but add hard safeguards: require signed or server-owned schemas; validate schemas against a strict allowlisted spec; sanitize markdown/HTML output with a proven sanitizer; lock plugin loading to vetted modules only; enforce backend validation/authorization for every submitted field regardless of UI visibility; harden upload endpoints (content sniffing, malware scan, quotas, storage isolation); and add audit logging for schema changes and plugin activation. If schema must be user-editable, run it through a policy engine that strips unsafe types/props before rendering.
- [adversarial_analyst] No changes are needed for the skill prompt itself. However, developers using this AI skill must ensure that any JSON output generated by the LLM is strictly validated and sanitized before being rendered in a user's browser. Pay special attention to URLs in file upload components and HTML/Markdown rendering to prevent XSS and malicious data routing.

### Analysis Group #6

- **Final Severity**: LOW
- **Confidence**: 72%
- **Is Malicious**: False
- **Is Gray Area**: True

#### Gray Area Analysis

## Gray Area Analysis

### Why this might be legitimate:
- This workflow is the standard release automation for the mithril-ui-form npm package. It triggers on pushes to main/master/beta/rc/alpha branches, runs semantic-release to handle versioning and changelog generation, and publishes the package to npm. This is a completely expected pattern for open-source npm package maintenance.
- The safe pattern for this kind of workflow is to have a single source of truth for publishing. If using `semantic-release`, let it fully control versioning, tagging, changelog, and npm publication via `@semantic-release/npm` and branch/channel configuration (e.g., stable on `main`, prereleases on `beta/alpha/rc`). This avoids fragile heuristics like checking whether `package.json` changed in `HEAD~1`. Correct usage also includes pinning action versions (or SHAs), using least-privilege tokens (`NPM_TOKEN` scoped to package publish only), and validating branch-to-channel mapping so pre-release branches cannot accidentally publish stable tags. In a monorepo, use package-specific change detection from workspace-aware tooling or semantic-release plugins, rather than generic git diff checks.
- This workflow is legitimately used to automate the semantic versioning, release creation, and npm publication process for the `mithril-ui-form` package whenever code is pushed to main release branches.

### Why this might be dangerous:
- The primary risk is supply chain: if `pnpm/action-setup@v4` (used with `version: latest` for pnpm) were compromised, it could intercept the NPM_TOKEN secret during the publish step. Additionally, the `id-token: write` permission combined with `contents: write` gives this workflow broad repository access. However, these are standard permissions for a release workflow and not indicative of malicious intent.
- This becomes dangerous when release logic is ambiguous or bypassable. Specific risky conditions: (1) `semantic-release` already publishes, then `pnpm publish` runs again, causing duplicate publish attempts, race conditions, or inconsistent dist-tags; (2) `git diff HEAD~1 --name-only | grep package.json` is a weak signal—merge commits, rebases, multi-commit pushes, or unrelated `package.json` edits can trigger/skip publish incorrectly; (3) if branch protections are weak, a contributor with push rights to `beta/rc/alpha` could trigger automated npm publication with `NPM_TOKEN`; (4) using broad triggers across multiple branches without strict semantic-release channel config can leak prerelease code to the wrong npm tag; (5) using floating action versions (e.g., `@v4`, `version: latest`) increases supply-chain drift risk. None of this indicates malware intent, but under these conditions it can cause accidental or unauthorized package distribution.
- To exploit this pipeline, an attacker would already need write access to the repository (e.g., compromised maintainer account or a successfully merged malicious PR). If they could modify the codebase, they could alter the `pnpm build` scripts to inject a malicious `prepublishOnly` hook into the `package.json`. When the subsequent `pnpm publish` step runs, the malicious hook would execute with the `NODE_AUTH_TOKEN` in its environment, allowing the attacker to exfiltrate the npm publishing token. However, this requires a prior compromise of repository access controls.

### Recommendations:
- [security_analyst] Pin `pnpm/action-setup@v4` to a specific pnpm version (e.g., `version: '9.x'`) instead of `latest` to avoid unexpected breaking changes or supply chain risk. Consider whether the double-publish step (semantic-release + pnpm publish) is intentional, as semantic-release typically handles npm publishing itself — the redundant publish step may cause issues. All other aspects of the workflow are standard and acceptable.
- [intent_analyst] Choose one publishing mechanism and remove the other. Preferred: keep `semantic-release` only, configure npm publishing and channels there, and delete the manual `pnpm publish` step. If manual publish must remain, replace `HEAD~1` checks with robust change detection (e.g., compare against last release tag or use Changesets/workspace filters), and gate publish with explicit conditions (`if: github.ref == 'refs/heads/main'` for stable, prerelease tags only on prerelease branches). Add branch protection + required reviews for release branches, use npm automation tokens with minimal scope/2FA policy, pin GitHub Actions to commit SHAs, and add concurrency controls (`concurrency: release-${{ github.ref }}`) to prevent parallel release races.
- [adversarial_analyst] The workflow is currently safe. For defense-in-depth, consider pinning third-party GitHub Actions (like `actions/checkout` and `pnpm/action-setup`) to specific commit SHAs rather than mutable major version tags (e.g., `@v4`) to protect against upstream action compromises. Ensure strict branch protection rules are enforced on all branches listed in the `on: push` trigger.

> This is a standard GitHub Actions CI/CD release workflow for an npm package. It checks out code, sets up Node.js and pnpm, builds packages, and publishes to npm using semantic-release. There is one minor concern: the workflow uses `pnpm/action-setup@v4` with `version: latest` rather than a pinned version, which could introduce supply chain risk if the action is compromised. Additionally, the double-publish pattern (semantic-release followed by a conditional `pnpm publish`) could theoretically publish a package twice or publish a version that wasn't properly versioned by semantic-release, but this is a workflow design issue rather than a security vulnerability. The use of `git diff HEAD~1` to detect version changes is a somewhat fragile heuristic. Overall, no malicious behavior is present.

The intent is legitimate CI/CD automation, but there is a gray-area reliability/safety issue: mixing `semantic-release` with a separate manual publish gate based on `git diff HEAD~1` can lead to incorrect publish decisions or duplicate/unintended publishes.

### Why this might be dangerous:
- This becomes dangerous under specific conditions: if similar debug configs are reused in shared/remote environments, users may unintentionally attach to or launch against non-local URLs; if `trace: true` is left on in sensitive environments, debug logs can reveal filesystem paths, module layout, or request details; if source-map overrides are broad or untrusted, they can cause misleading code mapping and mask what is actually executing; and if teams normalize stale "NOT WORKING" profiles, developers may launch incorrect targets and debug the wrong build artifacts, increasing risk of mistaken fixes or accidental exposure. While not an exploit by itself, the pattern can contribute to operational mistakes and information leakage when guardrails are absent.
- N/A. The file is benign. An attacker would need to modify this file (e.g., adding a malicious `preLaunchTask` or changing the browser executable path) to execute arbitrary code, which implies they already have write access to the developer's environment.

#### Gray Area Analysis

## Gray Area Analysis

### Why this might be legitimate:
- Injecting static SVG icons into the DOM for a documentation site generated by TypeDoc. Using innerHTML with hardcoded, trusted strings is a standard and safe way to quickly build DOM structures.
- TypeDoc generates this file as part of its API documentation output. The icons.js file embeds SVG icon definitions as a sprite sheet into the documentation page, allowing other parts of the documentation UI to reference these icons by ID (e.g., icon-1, icon-2, etc.) for TypeScript module, class, interface, function, and other type indicators in the generated docs.
- Using `innerHTML` can be acceptable when the HTML/SVG payload is fully static, hardcoded at build time, and never influenced by user input, URL params, API responses, localStorage, or other untrusted sources. In this pattern, safe use means: (1) the SVG string is a constant bundled with the app, (2) no runtime string concatenation with external data occurs, (3) the file is served from trusted origin under your deployment control, and (4) defense-in-depth controls exist (CSP, preferably Trusted Types) to prevent accidental future unsafe assignments. For this specific file, if it is auto-generated docs output and not editable by users, this is generally a low-risk implementation convenience.

### Why this might be dangerous:
- There is no viable abuse scenario here. An attacker cannot control the content being assigned to `innerHTML` because it is a hardcoded string literal without any variable interpolation.
- N/A - The innerHTML content is a fully static string literal with no user input, no external data, and no script tags or event handlers. There is no realistic XSS vector here. The only theoretical risk would be if an attacker could modify the source file itself, but that would require write access to the repository, at which point innerHTML is the least of concerns.
- This becomes dangerous if the injected SVG/HTML string becomes even partially attacker-controlled. Conditions that make it risky include: templating icon labels from untrusted data, loading icon markup from a CMS/API, query-string driven theme/icon overrides, compromised build pipeline, or third-party script mutation before assignment. In those cases, `innerHTML` could introduce executable payloads via SVG/HTML event handlers, malicious links, or script-capable constructs depending on browser parsing behavior and surrounding DOM usage. Missing safeguards for a potentially evolving codebase include explicit sanitization for any dynamic content, Trusted Types enforcement to block unsafe sinks, and a strict CSP to reduce exploitability if unsafe markup is introduced later.

### Recommendations:
- [adversarial_analyst] No action required. This is a false positive from the static analysis tool. If the scanner allows it, mark this specific instance or file path as an ignored false positive.
- [security_analyst] No action required. The static analysis tool flagged this as a false positive due to pattern matching on innerHTML assignment without considering that the assigned value is a fully static, hardcoded string literal with no user-controlled data. This is expected, safe behavior for a TypeDoc-generated documentation asset. If desired, the SVG content could be injected using createElementNS/appendChild calls instead, but this would be a cosmetic change with no security benefit in this context.
- [intent_analyst] Keep this as static-only content and document that requirement in code comments/tests. If any part may become dynamic, stop using raw `innerHTML`: build nodes with `createElementNS`/`setAttribute` or sanitize with a robust library (e.g., DOMPurify configured for SVG). Add defense-in-depth: enforce CSP (no inline script/eval), enable Trusted Types (`require-trusted-types-for 'script'`) with a narrow policy, and add a lint rule/test that forbids non-constant `innerHTML` assignments in this docs asset path.

**adversarial_analyst** (google/gemini-3.1-pro-preview):
> The code assigns a hardcoded string of SVG elements to the `innerHTML` of a dynamically created SVG element. Since there is no user input or external data interpolated into this string, there is absolutely no risk of Cross-Site Scripting (XSS). This is standard boilerplate code generated by documentation tools (like TypeDoc, indicated by the 'tsd-' classes) to embed icons into static documentation pages.

### Why this might be legitimate:
- This file is the standard JavaScript bundle generated by TypeDoc for rendering an API documentation website. It provides search functionality (via lunr.js), UI translations, and interactive documentation features like expand/collapse, copy buttons, and search indexing. This is expected and normal for any npm package that publishes API documentation.
- The only safe/legitimate use of `Function`-style dynamic evaluation is when all executed source is fully static, build-time generated, and never influenced by user input, URL params, remote content, or mutable storage. In this pattern, code should be frozen at build time, shipped as immutable assets, and protected with strong controls: strict CSP (preferably disallowing `unsafe-eval`), subresource integrity for CDN assets, dependency pinning, and reproducible builds. If a library requires `new Function`, it should be confined to non-sensitive contexts (e.g., docs site only), and documented as an explicit exception in security policy with periodic review.
- This is a standard asset file (main.js) generated by TypeDoc for rendering API documentation. It includes Lunr.js for client-side search functionality and translation strings for the documentation UI.

### Why this might be dangerous:
- N/A — There are no hardcoded external URLs for data exfiltration, no obfuscated payloads, no access to unrelated system resources, and no suspicious dynamic code execution. The Function() constructor flag from static analysis appears to be a false positive; the actual code shown uses standard arrow functions and module patterns, not dynamic Function() construction for malicious purposes.
- This becomes dangerous if any part of the string passed into `Function()` can be influenced by an attacker (directly or indirectly). Specific high-risk conditions include: loading search index/config/translations from untrusted sources; concatenating URL/query/hash values into dynamic code; compromised npm dependency or tampered build artifact; or enabling broad CSP allowances (`unsafe-eval`) in production apps. Under those conditions, an attacker can execute arbitrary JavaScript in the page context (XSS/RCE-in-browser equivalent), exfiltrate tokens/data, manipulate form content, or pivot to broader account/session compromise. Missing safeguards here are: explicit proof that evaluated strings are constant, CSP hardening, integrity verification of bundled assets, and guardrails that prevent dynamic code paths from consuming runtime data.
- None. This is static, client-side documentation code that does not process sensitive user input or execute dynamic strings as code.

- [security_analyst] No action required. This is a legitimate, auto-generated documentation asset. The static analysis finding is a false positive. Action items: (1) locate the exact `Function` call in the unminified source map/dependency tree; (2) confirm it does not consume runtime/untrusted input; (3) replace or reconfigure the dependency to avoid eval-like behavior if possible; (4) enforce CSP without `unsafe-eval` for production surfaces (or isolate docs on a separate origin if it must remain); (5) pin and audit dependencies and generated docs artifacts in CI; and (6) add a security test/lint rule that fails builds when new eval/Function usages appear without an approved exception.

## Trust Chain Analysis

**Overall Trust Verdict**: ⚠️ **VERIFY_BEFORE_USE**
*This is a legitimate UI form rendering library with no runtime network calls or financial operations, but downstream consumers should verify that the npm package has not been tampered with (ta_001), that the CI/CD pipeline's unpinned action tags have not been mutated (ta_003), and that the 'transform functions' feature does not execute arbitrary code from JSON schemas (ta_004) before integrating it into security-sensitive applications.*

This skill is a UI form generation library (mithril-ui-form) that converts JSON schemas into dynamic HTML forms using the Mithril.js framework. It performs no API calls, handles no blockchain/wallet operations, manages no credentials, and executes no financial transactions. The sensitive operations are limited to: (1) CI/CD pipeline publishing to npm using GitHub Actions secrets, and (2) rendering user-provided JSON form schemas into DOM elements. The primary trust dependencies are the integrity of the npm publishing pipeline (NPM_TOKEN secret), the trustworthiness of the GitHub Actions environment, and the safety of user-supplied JSON schemas that drive form rendering. Since this is a frontend form-rendering library with no network calls in its runtime code, the attack surface is narrow and well-understood. The main risk is supply-chain: a compromised npm publish could distribute malicious library code to downstream consumers.

*(Trust assumptions and verification details are shown at the top of this report.)*

### Data Flow Sources

| Category | Description | Source | Required Trust | Risk |
|----------|-------------|--------|----------------|------|
| Authorization | NPM_TOKEN secret used to publish packages to npm registry via GitHub Actions CI/CD pipeline | `env_variable` — .github/workflows/release.yml — env: NODE_AUTH_TOKEN: ${{ secrets.NPM_TOKEN }} and NPM_TOKEN: ${{ secrets.NPM_TOKEN }} | ta_001, ta_002 | 🟠 HIGH |
| Authorization | GITHUB_TOKEN used for creating GitHub releases, writing changelogs, and opening pull requests via semantic-release | `env_variable` — .github/workflows/release.yml — env: GITHUB_TOKEN: ${{ secrets.GITHUB_TOKEN }}; permissions: contents: write, issues: write, pull-requests: write, id-token: write | ta_002, ta_003 | 🟡 MEDIUM |
| User Input | JSON form schema provided by library consumers to define form structure, field types, conditional logic, and transform functions | `user_provided` — .claude/skills/mithril-ui-form.skill.md — form schema examples; README describes JSON-driven form generation with 'Transform functions for custom data processing' | ta_004 | 🟡 MEDIUM |
| User Input | Markdown content rendered within forms (markdown input and rendering feature) | `user_provided` — README — 'Markdown input and rendering' listed as a supported feature | ta_004, ta_005 | 🟡 MEDIUM |
| API Endpoints | No runtime API endpoints — the library makes no network calls during form rendering | `hardcoded` — No fetch/XHR/axios calls found in any provided source files. The library is purely a UI rendering framework. | — | 🟢 LOW |
| API Endpoints | npm registry endpoint used during CI/CD to publish packages (registry.npmjs.org) | `hardcoded` — .github/workflows/release.yml — uses actions/setup-node@v4 with registry-url: 'https://registry.npmjs.org' | ta_006 | 🟢 LOW |
| User Input | Drag-and-drop reordering of array form elements — user controls ordering of data objects | `user_provided` — README — 'Drag-and-drop reordering for arrays' feature in ArrayLayoutForm | — | 🟢 LOW |

### Manual Verification Checklist

> Items that cannot be auto-verified — check these before deploying this skill.

#### 1. [🔴 MUST] The GitHub Actions workflow pins third-party actions by tag (v4) rather than by commit SHA, which means tag mutation could introduce malicious code

**How to verify:** Check .github/workflows/release.yml — confirm actions/checkout@v4, actions/setup-node@v4, pnpm/action-setup@v4 are referenced by tag not SHA. Compare against official action repositories: github.com/actions/checkout, github.com/actions/setup-node, github.com/pnpm/action-setup to verify current v4 tags point to expected commits.

**If wrong:** ⚠️ If any action tag is moved to malicious code, that code runs in the CI environment with full access to NPM_TOKEN and GITHUB_TOKEN, enabling supply chain compromise of the published npm package

#### 2. [🔴 MUST] The mithril-ui-form package on npmjs.com (https://www.npmjs.com/package/mithril-ui-form) is maintained by the expected author (Erik Vullings) and has not had unauthorized versions published

**How to verify:** Visit https://www.npmjs.com/package/mithril-ui-form, check the maintainers list and version history. Verify the latest version matches the repository's CHANGELOG.md and git tags at github.com/erikvullings/mithril-ui-form

**If wrong:** ⚠️ An unauthorized version on npm would mean downstream consumers are installing potentially malicious code, constituting an active supply chain attack

#### 3. [🔴 MUST] The 'transform functions' feature in mithril-ui-form does not execute arbitrary JavaScript from JSON schema strings (no eval/Function constructor usage)

**How to verify:** Search the published package source at https://unpkg.com/mithril-ui-form/ or the GitHub repository packages/mithril-ui-form/src/ for usage of eval(), new Function(), setTimeout(string), or innerHTML assignments with schema-derived values

**If wrong:** ⚠️ Applications using mithril-ui-form with untrusted JSON schemas (e.g., schemas loaded from a database or user input) would be vulnerable to arbitrary JavaScript execution in the browser

#### 4. [🟡 SHOULD] The markdown rendering in mithril-ui-form uses a sanitizing markdown library (e.g., DOMPurify post-processing or a safe renderer like marked with sanitize option)

**How to verify:** Check packages/mithril-ui-form/package.json dependencies for markdown libraries (e.g., marked, markdown-it, showdown). Then verify in the source code that HTML output is sanitized before DOM insertion. Check for DOMPurify or similar sanitization.

**If wrong:** ⚠️ Applications rendering user-supplied markdown through mithril-ui-form forms would be vulnerable to stored/reflected XSS attacks

#### 5. [🟡 SHOULD] The id-token: write permission in the GitHub Actions workflow is actually required by semantic-release or npm publish for OIDC-based authentication

**How to verify:** Review semantic-release documentation at https://semantic-release.gitbook.io and npm provenance documentation at https://docs.npmjs.com/generating-provenance-statements to confirm whether id-token: write is needed. If not needed, it should be removed to reduce the blast radius of a compromised workflow.

**If wrong:** ⚠️ Unnecessary OIDC token generation capability increases the potential impact of a compromised workflow, as the token could be used to authenticate to cloud services or other OIDC-enabled systems

#### 6. [🔵 NICE TO HAVE] The pnpm-lock.yaml lockfile is committed and used with --frozen-lockfile in CI, ensuring dependency integrity

**How to verify:** Confirm pnpm-lock.yaml exists in the repository root (it does, per provided files) and that the CI workflow uses 'pnpm install --frozen-lockfile' (confirmed in release.yml). Verify the lockfile hashes match expected package contents by running 'pnpm audit' against the lockfile.

**If wrong:** ⚠️ Without a frozen lockfile, dependency resolution during CI could pull in unexpected or compromised package versions, potentially introducing malicious code into the build
